  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_56c195b2-d5a2-40d0-a266-5104fa434bac'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14286222779845100956">Antigone was originally produced in Paris in 1942, when France was an occupied nation and part of Hitler's Europe. The play depicts an authoritarian regime and the play's central character, the young Antigone, mirrored the predicament of the French people in the grips of tyranny. One of the masterpieces of the modern French stage.</span>

<div id="review_2484645523" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2484645523" />
    <a title="Fergus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus"><img alt="Fergus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567548184p2/70395042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2484645523?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fergus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fergus" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus">Fergus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2484645523" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11487005101521872063">TWO DIFFERENT WORLDS -<br/>WE LIVE IN TWO DIFFERENT WORLDS.<br/>1960's song.<br/><br/>If Sophocles' version of this is the best, Anouilh's is the one that's most like we all are INSIDE.<br/><br/>When I was 16, a callow youth, I thought Anouilh's heroine just couldn't compromise. I thought, how dumb! Creon was right to imprison her… But later in life, when my personal shibboleths were challenged by my seniors I wouldn't budge. <br/><br/>How come?<br/><br/>Well, our personal experiences in an amoral world can force our ethical hand, and then we c</span>
  <span id="freeText11487005101521872063" style="display:none">TWO DIFFERENT WORLDS -<br />WE LIVE IN TWO DIFFERENT WORLDS.<br />1960's song.<br /><br />If Sophocles' version of this is the best, Anouilh's is the one that's most like we all are INSIDE.<br /><br />When I was 16, a callow youth, I thought Anouilh's heroine just couldn't compromise. I thought, how dumb! Creon was right to imprison her… But later in life, when my personal shibboleths were challenged by my seniors I wouldn't budge. <br /><br />How come?<br /><br />Well, our personal experiences in an amoral world can force our ethical hand, and then we create personal principles. That’s the change that youth endures. We must FIND ourselves.<br /><br />And when we do, it’s: My way or the highway! <br /><br />Not all our moral choices come from upbringing, schooling, or culture. Some are created OURSELVES by our personal idiosyncrasies and histories. <br /><br />A traumatic childhood event can begin the personal conditioning process. So can parental abuse or sibling rivalry. Or, a sudden, earth-shaking shock. We are all different because of our private reactions as well as our parental conditioning. And especially today, because we are freer to choose. <br /><br />But are we luckier? Would Antigone be luckier today? No, because the law is the law, and Creon's law is the Law in Athens. As it is here now.<br /><br />Our freedom becomes a thorn in our side in the end with the endless conditions imposed on it. So we choose sides. And we naturally go for the most personally satisfying side. <br /><br />The other side chooses practical Necessity. Same thing: My way or the highway! <br /><br />But come, says the Law as the Lord says to Isaiah - let us reason together...<br /><br />YES. Let us Reason! For we can indeed lead with our heart - BUT WE MUST FOLLOW WITH OUR HEAD. Exactly as Creon says to Antigone…<br /><br />Like Antigone, and like all kids when faced with their seniors' ultimatums, reasoning is PASSIONATE. For Creon, and us adults, reasoning is HUMAN. It bends. As WE must bend, though not to the extent of committing a Wrong action. And Reason and our Heart TOGETHER will lead us to the Golden Mean.<br /><br />We must charitably bend. And that will Hurt.<br /><br />But you know, adults see kids as unreasonable, and kids sometimes see adults as middle-class moral mediocrities. The impasse isn't going to go away without the Balm of Time. <br /><br />Unfortunately neither Creon or Antigone HAS time! And, really, neither do we.<br /><br />It’s DOWN TO THE CRUNCH time! Time, as Auden says, to face the music:<br /><br />We would rather be ruined than changed<br />We would rather die in our dread<br />Than climb the cross of the moment<br />And let our illusions die.<br /><br />And we must face that blaring music over, and over again - without sacrificing our personal values. Our personal Cross will turn it to Harmony. And one day its “music will untune the sky.” On that day we will see our life as it really is.<br /><br />And we can finally Face the Face of truth. For it is Good...<br /><br />Anouilh's characters are as real as we are.<br /><br />And his play isn't about compromise, as I thought at 16. <br /><br />It's about living a real life among real people, WIDE AWAKE - with ALL our differences intact!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11487005101521872063" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_38366896" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38366896" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/38366896?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=french">french</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer38366896" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18104988050508920697"><i>[Original review, Nov 2008]</i><br/><br/>Read this play any time you feel you're being talked into something which sounds plausible, but which you know in your heart just stinks. It was written to encourage French people not to collaborate with the Nazis during the occupation. A miracle that it was ever performed. Were the Nazi censors simply stupid, or did some bureaucratic hero take a huge risk to get it cleared? Perhaps a bit of both... it's hard to see how just one would be enough. <br/><br/>The story is kind of he</span>
  <span id="freeText18104988050508920697" style="display:none"><i>[Original review, Nov 2008]</i><br /><br />Read this play any time you feel you're being talked into something which sounds plausible, but which you know in your heart just stinks. It was written to encourage French people not to collaborate with the Nazis during the occupation. A miracle that it was ever performed. Were the Nazi censors simply stupid, or did some bureaucratic hero take a huge risk to get it cleared? Perhaps a bit of both... it's hard to see how just one would be enough. <br /><br />The story is kind of heartbreaking. If you haven't fallen in love with Antigone by the end, you should probably seek medical attention.<br />_________________________<br /><br /><i>[Update, Sep 2020]</i><br /><br />I don't know why I didn't do this earlier, but I looked up the play <a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigone_(Anouilh)" rel="nofollow noopener">on French Wikipedia</a>. It's a very interesting article. Some of the more striking passages:<blockquote>« L'Antigone de Sophocle, lue et relue, et que je connaissais par cœur depuis toujours, a été un choc soudain pour moi pendant la guerre, le jour des petites affiches rouges. Je l'ai réécrite à ma façon, avec la résonance de la tragédie que nous étions alors en train de vivre. »<br /><br />— Jean Anouilh.<br /><br />[Sophocles' Antigone, read and reread, which I know by heart since forever, suddenly struck me with its force during the war, on the day of the little red posters. I rewrote it in my own way, with the resonance of the tragedy which we are now all in the middle of.]<br /><br />Anouilh se rappelle que « la salle était pleine tous les soirs, il y avait beaucoup d’officiers et de soldats allemands. Que pensaient-ils ? Plus perspicace, un écrivain allemand, Friedrich Sieburg, l’auteur de <i>Dieu est-il Français ?</i>, alerta, m’a-t-on dit, Berlin, disant qu’on jouait à Paris une pièce qui pouvait avoir un effet démoralisant sur les militaires qui s’y pressaient. Barsacq fut aussitôt convoqué à la Propagandastaffel où on lui fit une scène très violente, l’accusant de jouer une pièce sans avoir demandé l’autorisation. C’était grave. Barsacq fit l’imbécile innocent, la pièce avait été autorisée en 1941 – il montra son manuscrit tamponné et on retrouva le second exemplaire dans le bureau voisin. Les autorités allemandes ne pouvaient pas déjuger sans perdre la face. On lui suggéra cependant d’arrêter la pièce.<br /><br />[Anouilh remembers that "the theatre was full every evening, there were many German officers and soldiers. What were they thinking? Rather more alertly, a German author, Friedrich Sieburg, author of <i>Is God a Frenchman?</i> contacted, I have heard, Berlin, informing them that there was a play currently being performed in Paris which could have a demoralising effect on the members of the military who were attending in large numbers. Barsacq was summoned to appear before the Propagandastaffel where they gave him a very hard time, accusing him of producing a play without having requested permission. It was extremely serious. Barsacq did his best to appear innocent and dimwitted, explaining that the play had been duly authorized in 1941 - he showed his stamped copy of the manuscript and they found the duplicate copy in the adjoining office. The German authorities couldn't reverse the decision without losing face. All the same, they suggested to him that he should stop performing it.]</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="18104988050508920697" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1136073879" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1136073879" />
    <a title="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31461418-alienor-french-frowner"><img alt="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532688964p2/31461418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1136073879?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" href="/user/show/31461418-alienor-french-frowner">Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789990824490" href="/book/show/7727.Antigone">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=greek-myths">greek-myths</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=2015-read">2015-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=french-literature">french-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=reread">reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=classics-fr">classics-fr</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1136073879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1855417099518199638"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1437141825i/15542036._SX540_.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b>This is the most <i>French</i> retelling ever, y'all</b>. Think secular and pessimist. Because oh,<i> boy</i>, how depressing <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5228.Jean_Anouilh' title='Jean Anouilh' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jean Anouilh</a> was! Through the years his works showed more and more clearly how absurd human condition was for him, and how bitter he became. Yet <b>I love that guy</b> - there's just something so <i>liberating</i> oozing from this play. <br/><br/><blockquote>
  <b>"Et puis, surtout, c'est reposant, la tragédie, parce qu'on sait qu'il n'y a plus d'espoir, le sale espoir ; qu'on est pris, qu'on est enfin pris comme un rat, avec t</b></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText1855417099518199638" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1437141825i/15542036._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>This is the most <i>French</i> retelling ever, y'all</b>. Think secular and pessimist. Because oh,<i> boy</i>, how depressing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5228.Jean_Anouilh" title="Jean Anouilh" rel="nofollow noopener">Jean Anouilh</a> was! Through the years his works showed more and more clearly how absurd human condition was for him, and how bitter he became. Yet <b>I love that guy</b> - there's just something so <i>liberating</i> oozing from this play. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>"Et puis, surtout, c'est reposant, la tragédie, parce qu'on sait qu'il n'y a plus d'espoir, le sale espoir ; qu'on est pris, qu'on est enfin pris comme un rat, avec tout le ciel sur son dos, et qu'on n'a plus qu'à crier , - pas à gémir, non, pas à se plaindre, - à gueuler à pleine voix ce qu'on avait à dire, qu'on n'avait jamais dit et qu'on ne savait peut-être même pas encore."</b>
</blockquote><br>Stripped of any religious justifications, Antigone's choices are only this, <b>her</b> choices. They express her need to not compromise herself, perhaps, but above all, <b>her freedom to stand for herself and to make her own choices, to refuse to live in a world where her ideals can't help but break</b>. Stupid, Antigone? Maybe. But still amazing in her flaws. <br><br><b><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5228.Jean_Anouilh" title="Jean Anouilh" rel="nofollow noopener">Jean Anouilh</a>'s Antigone isn't a paragon of virtue</b>. She's flawed, detestable, infuriating, complex, oh so endearing. I love her, but I hate her a little. <br><br>As for <b>Creon</b>, he isn't the Tyrant of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1002.Sophocles" title="Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Sophocles</a> anymore, but the weak incarnation of politics shady deals. Bravo to Anouilh for making us feel - almost - bad for him.<br><br>Hemon, though. Poor, poor Hemon. <br><br><b>What is the most difficult? Saying no or saying yes?</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5228.Jean_Anouilh" title="Jean Anouilh" rel="nofollow noopener">Jean Anouilh</a> never says, but let the reader choose for himself, as his Antigone. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I'll say no, whatever stupid that can be. I love life, though. <i>See?</i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br><br>► Always a favorite. Love the writing, love the plot, love the characters, even when I hate them. I'll probably still do when I'll be 80. What impresses me the most is the fact that I <i>never</i> end thinking the same thing after each of my rereads - Here lies the strenght of this play. <br><br><b>
  <u>PREREVIEW</u>
</b><br>... And here we go for the reread of one of my absolute favorite when I was a teenager... Of course I'm worried, what do you think? <br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450702558i/17458088._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>My paperback shows its years &gt;.&lt; 1998! :O<br><br><i>For more of my reviews, please visit:</i><br><blockquote>
  <a href="https://meetthebookworld.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487851828i/22057164._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </a>
</blockquote></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2657804853126234023"><i>
  <b>“I spit on your happiness! I spit on your idea of life--that life that must go on, come what may. You are all like dogs that lick everything they smell. You with your promise of a humdrum happiness--provided a person doesn't ask much of life. I want everything of life, I do, and I want it now! I want it total, complete: otherwise, I reject it! I will not be moderate. I will not be satisfied with the bit of cake you offer me if I promise to be a good little girl. I want to be sure of everythin</b></i></span>
  <span id="freeText2657804853126234023" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>“I spit on your happiness! I spit on your idea of life--that life that must go on, come what may. You are all like dogs that lick everything they smell. You with your promise of a humdrum happiness--provided a person doesn't ask much of life. I want everything of life, I do, and I want it now! I want it total, complete: otherwise, I reject it! I will not be moderate. I will not be satisfied with the bit of cake you offer me if I promise to be a good little girl. I want to be sure of everything this very day; sure that everything will be as beautiful as when I was a little girl. If not, I want to die!”</b>
</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’And those who have survived will now begin quietly to forget the dead. They won’t remember who was who or which was which. It is all over. Antigone is calm tonight. She has played her part. (Three guards enter and begin to play cards.) A great melancholy wave of unrest peace now settles down upon Thebes, upon the empty palace, upon Creon, who can now begin to long for his own death. Only the guards are left, and none of this matters to them. It's no skin off their noses. They go on playing cards.’’</b>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=translation-is-impossible">translation-is-impossible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=fun-with-lara">fun-with-lara</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=too-sexy-for-maiden-aunts">too-sexy-for-maiden-aunts</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3619999879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8272368871837409243"><br/><b>NOT JUST ANOTHER GIRL-DOES-THE-RIGHT-THING-AND-GETS-KILLED-FOR-IT DRAMA, ALSO CONTAINS INTEGRATED AUDIO AND FRENCH SUBTITLES!</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1604216679i/30317887._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='95' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>It's my pleasure to present the most ambitious project yet attempted using the open source <a href='https://www.unige.ch/callector/lara/' rel='nofollow noopener'>LARA platform</a>: a full-length play, with each part recorded by a different person. Access it in <b>Chrome</b> or <b>Firefox</b> <a href='https://www.issco.unige.ch/en/research/projects/callector/antigone_en_rosa_kirstenvocabpages/_hyperlinked_text_.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>. You can appreciate it as a performance by just going through it and clicking on the speaker icons, but the intention is that francophones will also be able to use it</span>
  <span id="freeText8272368871837409243" style="display:none"><br><b>NOT JUST ANOTHER GIRL-DOES-THE-RIGHT-THING-AND-GETS-KILLED-FOR-IT DRAMA, ALSO CONTAINS INTEGRATED AUDIO AND FRENCH SUBTITLES!</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1604216679i/30317887._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="95" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>It's my pleasure to present the most ambitious project yet attempted using the open source <a href="https://www.unige.ch/callector/lara/" rel="nofollow noopener">LARA platform</a>: a full-length play, with each part recorded by a different person. Access it in <b>Chrome</b> or <b>Firefox</b> <a href="https://www.issco.unige.ch/en/research/projects/callector/antigone_en_rosa_kirstenvocabpages/_hyperlinked_text_.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>. You can appreciate it as a performance by just going through it and clicking on the speaker icons, but the intention is that francophones will also be able to use it to improve their English. If you hover over a pencil icon, you'll see a French translation of the preceding sentence, and if you hover over a word you'll see a translation of the word. Clicking on a word shows a concordance of places where the word appears in the text. If anyone feels like trying this out with a francophone school class, we'd be interested to hear what they think. I'm guessing it should be suitable for kids age 13 and over. I first read <i>Antigone</i> when I was in my early teens, and I immediately loved it. I've heard similar comments from several friends.<br><br>The cast who came together to record the play have done a terrific job, in particular the three leads: Rosa is very moving in the title role, Robert is powerful and credible as the king, and Gwyn's guard adds some great comic relief. The supporting roles aren't bad either. I had to listen to the whole thing several times while I was checking for technical glitches, and I never got bored. <br><br>In general, being involved in creating this unusual work of art was an amazing experience. We're also producing a French edition of the same play, with English translations - you'll find details posted <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3513573574" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>, the best parts are still available. If you speak good French, have a couple of evenings to spare, and fancy yourself as a doomed princess, a corrupt but interesting king or an oafish soldier, please contact us!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8272368871837409243" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <b>CHERCHONS: ROI CYNIQUE, GARDE COMIQUE, PRINCE TRAGIQUE</b>
</u><br/><br/>If you have a good francophone voice, you might enjoy spending an evening or two voicing a role in the virtual production of <i>Antigone</i> that we're putting together using the open source <a href='https://www.unige.ch/callector/lara/' rel='nofollow noopener'>LARA platform</a>.   <b>CHERCHONS: ROI CYNIQUE, GARDE COMIQUE, PRINCE TRAGIQUE</b>

<div id="review_1712723848" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1712723848" />
    <a title="Emma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28602001-emma"><img alt="Emma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438967931p2/28602001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1712723848?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emma" href="/user/show/28602001-emma">Emma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/27225034-antigone">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1712723848" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer567416421742657644">Anouilh has created a fascinating version of Antigone, but not one that I prefer to Sophocles'.<br/><br/>Instead of fighting to bury her brother because it is the right and just thing to do according to the eternal and unwritten rules of the gods, she is merely making a point, overwhelmed by youthful emotion, and one which in the final moments, she regrets. This is not the powerful Antigone of Greek Tragedy. Creon is not the hubristic, tyrannical King of old, he is a man forced to perform a difficult task</span>
  <span id="freeText567416421742657644" style="display:none">Anouilh has created a fascinating version of Antigone, but not one that I prefer to Sophocles'.<br /><br />Instead of fighting to bury her brother because it is the right and just thing to do according to the eternal and unwritten rules of the gods, she is merely making a point, overwhelmed by youthful emotion, and one which in the final moments, she regrets. This is not the powerful Antigone of Greek Tragedy. Creon is not the hubristic, tyrannical King of old, he is a man forced to perform a difficult task and one that he repeatedly tries to mediate himself out of. I found myself agreeing with several of his points and I usually detest Creon only slightly less than Agamemnon. <br /><br />The reworking of these characters against the backdrop of Nazi occupied France is something I find intriguing. That it had to pass the Nazi censors is only a small explanation for the changes within the play, especially as both sides could see something to champion in this version. Can't wait to delve more into this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="567416421742657644" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17080133605337167603">In the "note by the adapter" of the translation I read, he (Lewis Galantiere, who is, incidentally, a wonderful translator) related how Anouilh had to have the approval of the German censor in order for his play to open (in Paris in 1943). The fact that Anouilh was able to convey to the Germans a sense that Creon "wins" because he is lawfully "right," and to the French that in a sense Antigone's argument is the stronger in view of a higher power and moral "right" (and therefore comes as a call f</span>
  <span id="freeText17080133605337167603" style="display:none">In the "note by the adapter" of the translation I read, he (Lewis Galantiere, who is, incidentally, a wonderful translator) related how Anouilh had to have the approval of the German censor in order for his play to open (in Paris in 1943). The fact that Anouilh was able to convey to the Germans a sense that Creon "wins" because he is lawfully "right," and to the French that in a sense Antigone's argument is the stronger in view of a higher power and moral "right" (and therefore comes as a call for patriotism and revulsion against the Occupying forces) is truly brilliant and inspiring.</span>

<div id="review_3620810618" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3620810618" />
    <a title="Chadi Raheb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53414564-chadi-raheb"><img alt="Chadi Raheb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592047095p2/53414564.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3620810618?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chadi Raheb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chadi Raheb" href="/user/show/53414564-chadi-raheb">Chadi Raheb</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook " href="/book/show/55111309-antigone">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Whoever loves reading plays!</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Chadi by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53414564-chadi-raheb?shelf=litt-%CE%B5%CE%BB%CE%BB%CE%B7%CE%BD%CE%B9%CE%BA%CE%AC">litt-ελληνικά</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53414564-chadi-raheb?shelf=play">play</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53414564-chadi-raheb?shelf=fun-with-lara">fun-with-lara</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53414564-chadi-raheb?shelf=translation-in-french">translation-in-french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53414564-chadi-raheb?shelf=translation-in-english">translation-in-english</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3620810618" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5351883589542205992"><br/>If you've already read this fantastic play and wanna re-read it, it might be a great opportunity to hear it -while reading. It is now possible to listen to this play, read by a bunch of native English speakers, <a href='https://www.issco.unige.ch/en/research/projects/callector/antigone_en_rosa_kirstenvocabpages/_hyperlinked_text_.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a> on LARA. The translation is available in French -if you're also interested in French! Just hover the mouse over each word/sentence, et voilà!<br/><br/><br/><br/>P.S.<br/>There's also the French Edition of Antigone <a href='https://www.issco.unige.ch/en/research/projects/callector/365_Antigonevocabpages/_hyperlinked_text_.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a> , but as you can see/hear it's not completely done yet. We still need some more volunteers</span>
  <span id="freeText5351883589542205992" style="display:none"><br />If you've already read this fantastic play and wanna re-read it, it might be a great opportunity to hear it -while reading. It is now possible to listen to this play, read by a bunch of native English speakers, <a href="https://www.issco.unige.ch/en/research/projects/callector/antigone_en_rosa_kirstenvocabpages/_hyperlinked_text_.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a> on LARA. The translation is available in French -if you're also interested in French! Just hover the mouse over each word/sentence, et voilà!<br /><br /><br /><br />P.S.<br />There's also the French Edition of Antigone <a href="https://www.issco.unige.ch/en/research/projects/callector/365_Antigonevocabpages/_hyperlinked_text_.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a> , but as you can see/hear it's not completely done yet. We still need some more volunteers for some roles. You can read more about it <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3513573574?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a> on <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1834894.Manny_Rayner" rel="nofollow noopener">Manny</a>'s review. And if you know French and are interested in recording some lines (and believe me, it's so easy and enjoyable!), please leave a comment under Manny's review -or mine if it's easier :)<br /><br /><br />P.P.S.<br />I’m <i>Ismène</i> in French edition btw ☺️<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1663884444" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1663884444" />
    <a title="Alice Poon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28700577-alice-poon"><img alt="Alice Poon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499099841p2/28700577.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1663884444?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alice Poon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alice Poon" href="/user/show/28700577-alice-poon">Alice Poon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782710300250" href="/book/show/530961.Antigone">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28700577-alice-poon?shelf=french-authored">french-authored</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1663884444" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15291685288606260825"><br/>Very intense and affecting dialogues. The play pits idealism against realism in the form of heated arguments between Antigone and her uncle King Creon. By presenting Antigone as being almost naive and pigheadedly irrational and Creon as tenuously considerate and reasonable, Anouilh exposes the real pith of both characters. In truth, Antigone is the epitome of the perfectionist idealist, whereas King Creon represents the hypocritical and callous tyrant whose only concern is power and politics.<br/><br/>In </span>
  <span id="freeText15291685288606260825" style="display:none"><br />Very intense and affecting dialogues. The play pits idealism against realism in the form of heated arguments between Antigone and her uncle King Creon. By presenting Antigone as being almost naive and pigheadedly irrational and Creon as tenuously considerate and reasonable, Anouilh exposes the real pith of both characters. In truth, Antigone is the epitome of the perfectionist idealist, whereas King Creon represents the hypocritical and callous tyrant whose only concern is power and politics.<br /><br />In the commentary, it is mentioned that some critics interpret the play as apologist for the Vichy regime in Nazi-occupied France by reading pragmatism and circumspection into the character of Creon. My own conclusion is that that is hardly what the author intended.<br /><br />I'm giving the play 4 stars.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15291685288606260825" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1662958545" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1662958545" />
    <a title="Leslie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11351200-leslie"><img alt="Leslie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391045942p2/11351200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1662958545?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leslie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leslie" href="/user/show/11351200-leslie">Leslie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782710300250" href="/book/show/530961.Antigone">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11351200-leslie?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11351200-leslie?shelf=french">french</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1662958545" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13563470715126750296">Read this French text while listening to an English-language audiobook. (translated by Christopher Nixon).<br/><br/>Luckily for me I got the audiobook as when I turned to the print edition I had checked out from the library, it turned out to be in French!! My French isn't good enough to have read this alone but was good enough to attempt reading it with the help of an English translation in audio :) It was an interesting experience! The L.A. TheatreWorks audiobook doesn't include stage directions so I wou</span>
  <span id="freeText13563470715126750296" style="display:none">Read this French text while listening to an English-language audiobook. (translated by Christopher Nixon).<br /><br />Luckily for me I got the audiobook as when I turned to the print edition I had checked out from the library, it turned out to be in French!! My French isn't good enough to have read this alone but was good enough to attempt reading it with the help of an English translation in audio :) It was an interesting experience! The L.A. TheatreWorks audiobook doesn't include stage directions so I would pause momentarily while I read these.<br /><br />One thing that I noticed is that while Creon talks to Antigone in the familiar (tu), she responds to him in the formal (vous). This difference gives a spin to their relationship which cannot easily be duplicated in English.<br /><br />Reading this knowing that it was written &amp; first performed in Vichy France gives certain phrases and actions a special significance. However, even without that Anouilh's version of this story had some interesting twists to Sophocles' original. Creon is a more ambivalent character; he seems more reasonable, more caring and less stubborn than the one in either the Sophocles or Heaney versions. Antigone's relationships with Haemon (Creon's son) and her sister Ismene are both expanded but her motivation for her actions in this version is much more murky. By lessening the contrast between the 2 characters you would expect that the tension would be less but Anouilh manages to make their confrontation even more heartbreaking as it has overtones of a family feud (and of course, if you read into it Creon as the French colloborator acting for the Nazis and Antigone as the Resistance fighter, then the drama is heightened even further).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13563470715126750296" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_30516900" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30516900" />
    <a title="C." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1425694-c"><img alt="C." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1245484320p2/1425694.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30516900?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="C." class="user" itemprop="url" name="C." href="/user/show/1425694-c">C.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782710300250" href="/book/show/530961.Antigone">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=own-or-access">own-or-access</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=antigone">antigone</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30516900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6843957757115689366">I think I'm becoming a little bit obsessed with this play, which is kind of fun. It's at the point now where I am actually very keen to learn Ancient Greek so I can read the original Sophocles. In the meantime, though, I would like to read an English translation of Anouilh, and then read the French again, or perhaps read them concurrently. <br/><br/>This was very interesting, not least because all the data points fitted. I think just about everything from Sophocles' original <i>Antigone</i> was included: every c</span>
  <span id="freeText6843957757115689366" style="display:none">I think I'm becoming a little bit obsessed with this play, which is kind of fun. It's at the point now where I am actually very keen to learn Ancient Greek so I can read the original Sophocles. In the meantime, though, I would like to read an English translation of Anouilh, and then read the French again, or perhaps read them concurrently. <br /><br />This was very interesting, not least because all the data points fitted. I think just about everything from Sophocles' original <i>Antigone</i> was included: every character inconsistency, every strange piece of dialogue. This is quite impressive, as there are a lot of odd character traits in Sophocles' version, which is partly what makes it great; one can never quite be sure what the characters' motives are.<br /><br />Anouilh's Antigone (the character) does not at first appear to be the headstrong, stubborn, impetuous, temperamental young woman of Sophoclean fame. She seems quiet, demure, and her actions seem well thought-through. She seems to understand why Creon feels it necessary to leave Polynices' body rotting in public, and her actions seem not to be motivated so much by an incestuous obsession with her brother as by a true dedication to the cause of justice. <br /><br />At first I was rather disappointed by this; Sophocles' characters act irrationally, which is more fun, and also I think more realistic. But eventually I realised that so do Anouilh's; Antigone is above all an unreliable narrator, and by the end it is clear that she is perhaps just as mad and death-obsessed as she is in Sophocles. In fact, in this version Creon comes across as by far the more reasonable; the more detailed exposition of Polynices and Eteocles' characters and actions made it much more difficult to sympathise with Polynices and Antigone (and I suspect their actions were taken directly from Ancient Greek accounts, so exactly the same thing would have happened in Sophocles' Antigone, just not mentioned in the play). <br /><br />I found that in this specific instance, it is difficult not to understand Creon's actions: Polynices and Eteocles were both absolute bastards trying to unseat their father from the throne so that they could be ruler of Thebes; both died in battle while fighting each other; Creon chose at random one to be buried in state and the other to be left to rot as an example for the populace. This makes a lot of sense to me, and given how terrible their actions were I can understand why a ruler would want to do such a thing. This is not really made clear in Sophocles. But when you think about it on a more abstract level, this is bad, bad, bad! No ruler should deprive any citizen of their rights for any reason, no matter how horrible they are, no matter how much they harm the state! So interesting.<br /><br />I question the wisdom of leaving out Tireisias and his prediction, and Creon's attempts to undo what he has done. In Sophocles, this has the effect of evening out the responsibility, because Creon redeems himself in a sense but is still punished harshly by the deaths of his son and wife. In a way, though, it makes sense, because then both Creon and Antigone stick stubbornly to their beliefs until the end. Also possibly it helped get this play past the German censorship.<br /><br />The portrayal of the sentry was very interesting. In Sophocles' version, the sentry is funny, witty, clever, compassionate. He acts as a foil to Creon; ironically so, since he displays the qualities of 'noble blood' much more clearly than Creon ever does. Anouilh's sentry, however, is an imbecile; morally stunted, stupid, ignorant, uneducated. A much more accurate portrayal of a sentry, in other words. And he does still provide comic relief! The scene where Antigone is trying to dictate her final letter to Hémon is both hilarious and tragic.<br /><br />Manny makes much of the beautiful writing in this play, and I did like a lot of it - simple language, but used well. I did feel like a lot of the arguments between Antigone and Creon went a bit crazy, though. Around and around in circles, constantly - they reminded me of nothing so much as my exchange partner arguing with her mother: "Mais si!" "Mais non!" "Mais si!" "Mais non! Tais toi!" "Non!" and so on and so on, ad infinitum. And then Antigone came up with this zinger of a line: - "Oui, je suis laide! C'est ignoble, n'est-ce pas, ces cris, ces sursauts, cette lutte de chiffoniers." And later, "C'est trop laid, tout cela, tout est trop laid." And I loved that about this version: everything in Sophocles seems very noble and virtuous - everything takes place on a level above that of normal huamn activity,everything has a kind of tragic beauty. Anouilh's version was so much more earthy, more realistic. There is really nothing beautiful about a situation like this, and there was no idealisation of anything. Which was beautifully mirrored by the simple, everyday language - none of Sophocles' poetic flights of fancy for Anouilh.<br /><br />The thing which is most important about <i>Antigone</i>, though, is retained by Anouilh. You're not supposed to be able to choose between Creon and Antigone; the Ancient Greek audience wasn't supposed to either. They are both wrong and they are both right, and this we should never forget.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6843957757115689366" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1356257380" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1356257380" />
    <a title="Dov Zeller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/440651-dov-zeller"><img alt="Dov Zeller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478090196p2/440651.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1356257380?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dov Zeller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dov Zeller" href="/user/show/440651-dov-zeller">Dov Zeller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440651-dov-zeller?shelf=theater">theater</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440651-dov-zeller?shelf=wwii-related">wwii-related</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1356257380" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14991124043266772097">I am all of a sudden fascinated with theater during the occupation of France (thanks Cathy!).<br/><br/>Last night I read Antigone and there are a lot of things to explore in this play, but one thing that's really interesting to me is that it was written during the occupation and there is all this mythology surrounding it. There are accepted fables, that this is an anti-occupation play -- a play that was written slyly enough that it got past the German censors even though it had an anti-occupation message.</span>
  <span id="freeText14991124043266772097" style="display:none">I am all of a sudden fascinated with theater during the occupation of France (thanks Cathy!).<br /><br />Last night I read Antigone and there are a lot of things to explore in this play, but one thing that's really interesting to me is that it was written during the occupation and there is all this mythology surrounding it. There are accepted fables, that this is an anti-occupation play -- a play that was written slyly enough that it got past the German censors even though it had an anti-occupation message. <br /><br />The truth is a lot more complicated. Apparently right after the occupation ended revisionist stories started flying around, claiming that all of the French writers/playwrights (and audience members)... had been anti-occupation (heroes). But it wasn't quite like that. A lot of writers, and a whole lot of people (among those who could), went along with the occupational forces, and along with their lives, and a lot of playwrights were taking advantage of theater culture during the occupation and just trying to make a buck. <br /><br />So it is not necessarily true that Anouilh was anti-occupation. (I am still doing some research into his politics and motivations. Hard to get access to some of the more thoroughly researched academic materials.) What I was able to find out was that just about anyone who saw the play at the time of the occupation seemed to see it as a play that supported their particular positions (and yet, perhaps, also as a play that brought their positions into question?) It is certainly true that the occupation was on Anouilh's mind when he wrote Antigone. This quote made sense to me.<br /><br />“If Anouilh’s Antigone comes closer to the universality of the greeks than do most other modern re-creations of myths, it is not only because of the inherent expressiveness of the myth and the author’s ability to see below the rationalized surface of the story. In addition to these important factors, we should remember that Antigone was written and produced during the Occupation. Even if we do not believe Anouilh intended it as a thrust at the occupiers and at Vichy, we must still recognize that he could rely on a heightened sensitivity in his audience, and a feeling of a collective religious kind of experience before a spectacle depicting the struggle for individual liberty and showing the physically vanquished as essentially free. <br /><br />from “The World of Jean Anouilh" By Leonard Cabell Pronko<br /><br />In any case, it's a disturbing play. Perhaps even more disturbing than the Greek tragedy, because of the heightened calmness, the perfectly reasonable discussions about terribly, frighteningly unreasonable things. <br /><br />It's hard for me to imagine this play in production. It is a lot of talk, and not much of anything else. I think it comes very close to prose and I wonder if that is customary for the time/tradition he is writing in, or if it is unusual. <br /><br />I don't feel comfortable rating this one. I guess I don't have to give things stars? Hmmmm.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14991124043266772097" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1994694470" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1994694470" />
    <a title="Jenny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7206839-jenny"><img alt="Jenny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593734236p2/7206839.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1994694470?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny" href="/user/show/7206839-jenny">Jenny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782710330400" href="/book/show/3895434-antigone">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1994694470" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4705014334334835071">I don't really know how to write about this play. It's Antigone through the lens of World War II. It's roughly the same story as Sophocles' original, but it's told so differently and with such a different heart. Anouilh was in pain, and he was trying to sort out the world that he once knew and the world he was now forced to live in. Like his Antigone, he could no longer see life the way he used to, but he couldn't accept it as it is now. It's the tragedy that he talks about and the drama. What h</span>
  <span id="freeText4705014334334835071" style="display:none">I don't really know how to write about this play. It's Antigone through the lens of World War II. It's roughly the same story as Sophocles' original, but it's told so differently and with such a different heart. Anouilh was in pain, and he was trying to sort out the world that he once knew and the world he was now forced to live in. Like his Antigone, he could no longer see life the way he used to, but he couldn't accept it as it is now. It's the tragedy that he talks about and the drama. What he lived through was real, and he had to go through it, but in doing so, he accepted it for what it was. The aftermath is the drama--it doesn't feel real anymore, and he can't have hope because of what he's seen, so what's left? <br />I like that Antigone and Créon, youth and age, the follower of the law and the law maker, vie against each other, but each have equally beautiful and equally powerful lines. Anouilh doesn't seem to favor one view over the other. In Sophocles' play, Creon is clearly the villain. Antigone is the hero. But this play is very different, and I understand why. It's not about the afterlife, religion versus politics, honor versus obedience. It's about world views, about death, about the people who have to say "yes" because they drive the boat and the people who can say "no" because they don't have to worry about where the boat is going. <br />It's such a good play. It's well-paced, well-written, and brave. It's a portrait of a time told through the picture of an older and, in some ways, a simpler time. Anouilh found a way to relate what he was feeling with a play that he'd always known and loved. It's about the power of drama and literature to move us but also to allow room for expression. And sometimes, retelling stories is the best way to share our own. <br />I highly recommend this play. I don't know how it would be in English, but I'm sure the power and passion would translate just like it does from Sophocles' Antigone. Good writing is good writing in any language, and emotions are universal.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4705014334334835071" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_265306208" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/265306208" />
    <a title="Ashley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4554936-ashley"><img alt="Ashley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597527762p2/4554936.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/265306208?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ashley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ashley" href="/user/show/4554936-ashley">Ashley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer265306208" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11450067895179892897">This used to be my favorite play in the entire world. I think that was because I had identified with Anouillh's Antigone much more when I was very young and very self-righteous. Maybe I'm still both of those things, but that's got nothing to do with this play so I will just say, upon re-reading this, that it is a really good adaptation of Sophocles. An excellent one, in fact. The real star of this adaptation, I now realize, is Creon. He is still 'un tyrant' but a very complex one who sees himsel</span>
  <span id="freeText11450067895179892897" style="display:none">This used to be my favorite play in the entire world. I think that was because I had identified with Anouillh's Antigone much more when I was very young and very self-righteous. Maybe I'm still both of those things, but that's got nothing to do with this play so I will just say, upon re-reading this, that it is a really good adaptation of Sophocles. An excellent one, in fact. The real star of this adaptation, I now realize, is Creon. He is still 'un tyrant' but a very complex one who sees himself as master of his people, yes, but "under and not above the law" of his country. This guy was palatable to the French resistance and the Nazi occupiers alike so I'm sure, like most good politicians, he will be able to get most readers to at least see some validity in his point of view.<br /><br />Antigone is less pitiable, perhaps, in this version than in Sophocles'. She is stubborn and entirely unable to see reason. Perhaps her rationale for burying Polynices carried some weight in the original play, but in this 20th century version she really has no Gods or true filial duty to invoke so she comes off more as a woman with a death wish. But all of Oedipus' family seemed to have a death wish, so I digress. Antigone's a fantastic character all the same and some of the lines that she and the chorus are given to recite are very beautifully worded. My favorite being: "Yes, I am ugly! Father was ugly, too. But Father became beautiful. And do you know when? At the very end. When all his questions had been answered."<br /><br />Basically the reasoning behind Antigone's actions and the core message that her life and death brings to the play is this: "What a person can do, a person should do." The French likely identified with her self-sacrifice in the name of human decency, something which was surely lacking in a nation overrun with Nazis and the nasty French fascist terror squads, which got pretty good representation as the three gross guards who manhandled the play's heroine. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11450067895179892897" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13261443938817910653">I'm not usually a fan of adaptions of classical texts, but this was <i>fantastic</i>. It keeps all the spirit of Sophocles' original, while being highly relevant to the period it was produced in (Nazi occupied France). Antigone clearly represents the French Resistance and the unthinking Guards Hitlers' Nazi followers. What's interesting is the representation of the harsh and unyielding ruler Creon (Hitler). The performance was allowed by the Nazi regime because the portrayal of the king was complex and</span>
  <span id="freeText13261443938817910653" style="display:none">I'm not usually a fan of adaptions of classical texts, but this was <i>fantastic</i>. It keeps all the spirit of Sophocles' original, while being highly relevant to the period it was produced in (Nazi occupied France). Antigone clearly represents the French Resistance and the unthinking Guards Hitlers' Nazi followers. What's interesting is the representation of the harsh and unyielding ruler Creon (Hitler). The performance was allowed by the Nazi regime because the portrayal of the king was complex and ambiguous, emphasising the difficulties of ruling and the tough decisions he has to make, rather than portraying him as an outright tyrant. Though I don't know how much of an innovation this is on Anouilh's part; Sophocles' original Creon would have been at least somewhat sympathetic to the Athenian audience. Because his views of citizenship and the duties of the citizens (including putting the polis/state before philoi/loved-ones) were 'right' in the view of classical Athenians. But that's exactly where the tragedy arises in both versions of the play - the conflict between state and family. </span>

<div id="review_3538485838" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3538485838" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48011083-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542289434p2/48011083.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1848742952154009531">Another of those works I glossed over in high school English class, to revisit with intent as a much older adult, Jean Anouilh’s <i>Antigone</i> is a provocative work for its era, occupied France in the Second World War. Even today, there are parallels between Antigone’s dedication to family honor and the spirit embedded in more recent popular movements. I’m confused, however, why, when presented with Creon’s version of the relationship among her brothers, Polynices and Eteocles, and her father, Oedipu</span>
  <span id="freeText1848742952154009531" style="display:none">Another of those works I glossed over in high school English class, to revisit with intent as a much older adult, Jean Anouilh’s <i>Antigone</i> is a provocative work for its era, occupied France in the Second World War. Even today, there are parallels between Antigone’s dedication to family honor and the spirit embedded in more recent popular movements. I’m confused, however, why, when presented with Creon’s version of the relationship among her brothers, Polynices and Eteocles, and her father, Oedipus, Antigone’s death wish remained. If true, was not her antagonism to Creon ultimately in error?</span>

<div id="review_887036728" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/887036728" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12941010-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502566869p2/12941010.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/887036728?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/12941010-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12941010-chris?shelf=francais">francais</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12941010-chris?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12941010-chris?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer887036728" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12259006384156561368">If you like your literature to feature strong, articulate women you don’t have to go much farther than Antigone. Jean Anouilh’s version was written in 1941-1942 at the height of the Nazi occupation of Paris. Creon is the evil tyrant and Antigone is the voice of resistance. <br/><br/>But is it that simple? Jean Anouilh has cleverly written his play in such a way that we can almost sympathise with both sides. <br/><br/>The war is over and Creon is triumphant. Antigone’s brothers, part of the revolt, are both dead. Cr</span>
  <span id="freeText12259006384156561368" style="display:none">If you like your literature to feature strong, articulate women you don’t have to go much farther than Antigone. Jean Anouilh’s version was written in 1941-1942 at the height of the Nazi occupation of Paris. Creon is the evil tyrant and Antigone is the voice of resistance. <br /><br />But is it that simple? Jean Anouilh has cleverly written his play in such a way that we can almost sympathise with both sides. <br /><br />The war is over and Creon is triumphant. Antigone’s brothers, part of the revolt, are both dead. Creon decides that Etéocle should have a proper burial as befits the son of Oedipus but that Polynice’s body should be left to rot. Antigone cannot accept that one of her brother’s is not entitled to a proper burial. After all, Polynice is also the son of Oedipus.<br /><br />She makes feeble efforts to bury Polynice and for this, the guards bring her before Creon. In the latter part of the play there is a powerful, engaging debate between the two of them. For Creon, it is simple. There is a law, he is King and Antigone must obey, no matter who her father is. For Antigone, it is equally simple. There is a law that her brother should be buried with all proper ceremony, no matter what treachery he is accused of committing.<br /><br />Some have argued that Antigone is needlessly provocative, that she was just looking for an excuse to die. There’s little doubt she could have saved herself. Why was it so important for her to resist Creon over a small thing like a burial? She was young, foolhardy and looking for a fight. Maybe, living in the 21st century, we cannot appreciate how important something like a proper burial was to communities then. Even for someone like Antigone who didn’t really believe.<br /><br />Clearly, had we been in Antigone’s position, most of us would have looked for a compromise. But sometimes the only way forward for someone so passionate about her beliefs, is to be prepared to give up her life.<br /><br />Did Bobby Sands want to die? Did Jan Palach want to die? Would there have been an Arab Spring without the Tunisian Mohamed Bouazizi giving up his life? Would the English have been defeated without Jeanne d’arc?<br /><br />If the brave men and women of the French Resistance hadn’t fought and suffered horrific losses, where today would be the soul of France. Where would its pride be if the maquis hadn’t been prepared to say no to the horrors of the Nazi occupation. Every now and then we need an Antigone.<br /><br />She had so much to live for and in the first half of the book she makes it clear that she has no desire to die. But if Antigone had acquiesced could she have continued to live with herself? <br /><br />It’s easy to make excuses for Creon. Isn’t he the reasonable one? He offers Antigone plenty of opportunities to back down. His authority would be undermined if he, the King, gave in and that would be no good for the people of Thebes. Creon's just doing his job. He just wants a quiet life and to get on with the business of ruling. That wasn’t possible then, and it wasn’t possible for the likes of that other war hero, Pétain. The consequences of ruthless tyranny are played out at the end of this drama - after Creon kills Antigone, he next loses his son and his wife. You may see Creon as being oh so reasonable. You may see Antigone as a fiery young woman, too idealistic for her own good. But look where it leads when Creon fails to appreciate the power of Antigone’s pleadings.<br /><br />The consequences of failing to stand up to tyranny are worse than collaborating with it. Collaboration with violent injustice is never the answer - for either side.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12259006384156561368" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_248478676" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/248478676" />
    <a title="תניה" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5767056"><img alt="תניה" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532204992p2/5767056.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/248478676?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="תניה" class="user" itemprop="url" name="תניה" href="/user/show/5767056">תניה</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5767056?shelf=4-star-wonders">4-star-wonders</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer248478676" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4883441556695726875">Antigone: Understand! The first word I ever heard out of any of you was that word "understand". Why didn't I "understand" that I must not play with water - cold, black, beautiful flowing water - because I'd spill it on the palace tiles. Or with earth, because earth dirties a little girl's frock. Why didn't I "understand" that nice children don't eat out of every dish at once; or give everything in their pockets to beggars; or run in the wind so fast that they fall down; or ask for a drink when t</span>
  <span id="freeText4883441556695726875" style="display:none">Antigone: Understand! The first word I ever heard out of any of you was that word "understand". Why didn't I "understand" that I must not play with water - cold, black, beautiful flowing water - because I'd spill it on the palace tiles. Or with earth, because earth dirties a little girl's frock. Why didn't I "understand" that nice children don't eat out of every dish at once; or give everything in their pockets to beggars; or run in the wind so fast that they fall down; or ask for a drink when they're perspiring; or want to go swimming when it's either too early or too late, merely because they happen to feel like swimming. Understand! I don't want to understand. There'll be time enough to understand when I'm old... If I ever <i>am</i> old. But not now. (p.19/20)<br /><br />Ismene: His mob will come running, howling as it runs. A thousand arms will seize our arms. A thousand breaths will breathe into our faces. Like one single pair of eye, a thousand eyes will stare at us. We'll be driven in a tumbrel through their hatred, through the smell of them and their cruel, roaring laughter. We'll be dragged to the scaffold for torture, surrounded by guards with their idiot faces all bloated, their animal hands clean-washed for the sacrifice, their beefy eyes squinting as they stare at us. And we'll know that no shrieking and no begging will make them understand that we want to live, for they are like slaves who do exactly as they've been told, without caring about right or wrong. And we shall suffer, we shall feel pain rising in us until it becomes so unbearable that we <i>know</i> it must stop. But it won't stop; it will go on rising and rising, like a screaming voice. (p.20)<br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4883441556695726875" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
